  <div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Tamang_language" title="Tamang language">Tamang</a></li></ul></div>\n</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <b><a href="/wiki/Uzbek_language" title="Uzbek language">Uzbek</a></li></ul></div>\n</li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Time zones</th><td><div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border: none; srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_India.svg/35px-Flag_of_India.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_India.svg/45px-Flag_of_India.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>\n</li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/UN_M.49" title="UN M.49">UN M.49 code</a></th><td><code>034</code> &#8211; Southern Asia<br/><code>142</code> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a><br/><code>001</code> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">World</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>South Asia</b> or <b>Southern Asia</b>, is a term used to represent the <a href="/wiki/South" title="South">southern</a> <a href="/wiki/Region" title="Region">region</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian</a> <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continent</a>, which comprises the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">sub-Himalayan</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SAARC" title="SAARC">SAARC</a> countries and, <a href="#Definition_by_South_Asian_Studies_programs">for some authorities</a>, adjoining countries to the west and east. Topographically, it is dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian Plate</a>, which rises <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" title="Above mean sea level">above sea level</a> as <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> and northern parts of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> situated south of the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a>. South Asia is bounded on the south by the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> and on land (clockwise, from <a href="/wiki/West" title="West">west</a>) by <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">West Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>.\n</p><p>The current territories of <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> form South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unreports.studycenters_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-unreports.studycenters-3">[3]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_Association_for_Regional_Cooperation" title="South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation">South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation</a> (SAARC) is an economic cooperation organisation in the region which was established in 1985 and includes all eight nations comprising South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SAARC_Summit_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-SAARC_Summit-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>South Asia covers about 5.2 million km<sup>2</sup> (2 million mi<sup>2</sup>), which is 11.71% of the Asian continent or 3.5% of the world\'s land surface area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unreports.studycenters_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-unreports.studycenters-3">[3]</a></sup> The population of South Asia is about 1.891 billion or about one fourth of the world\'s population, making it both the <a href="/wiki/List_of_continents_by_population#Regional_and_continental_(sub)totals_in_2016" title="List of continents by population">most populous</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">most densely populated</a> geographical region in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-South_Asia_Regional_Overview_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-South_Asia_Regional_Overview-5">[5]</a></sup> Overall, it accounts for about 39.49% of Asia\'s population, over 24% of the world\'s population, and is home to a vast array of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JJCO_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-JJCO-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoMA_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-EoMA-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2010, South Asia had the world\'s largest population of <a href="/wiki/Hindus" title="Hindus">Hindus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sikhs" title="Sikhs">Sikhs</a>. It also has the largest population of Muslims in the <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific" title="Asia-Pacific">Asia-Pacific</a> region,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pewforum.org_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-pewforum.org-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> as well as over 35 million <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> and 25 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhists" title="Buddhists">Buddhists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pew_Research_2012_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Research_2012-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#References"><span class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:South_Asia_UN.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3588" data-file-width="2960" decoding="async" height="424" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/South_Asia_UN.png/350px-South_Asia_UN.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/South_Asia_UN.png/525px-South_Asia_UN.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/South_Asia_UN.png/700px-South_Asia_UN.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:South_Asia_UN.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartographic</a> map of South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unmapsworld_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-unmapsworld-12">[12]</a></sup> However, the United Nations does not endorse any definitions or area boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[note 1]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The total area of South Asia and its geographical extent is not clear cut as systemic and foreign policy orientations of its constituents are quite asymmetrical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghosh1989_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ghosh1989-14">[13]</a></sup> Aside from the central region of South Asia, formerly part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, there is a high degree of variation as to which other countries are included in South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Razzaque3_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Razzaque3-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmann_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-mmann-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson5_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-anderson5-18">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Modern definitions of South Asia are consistent in including Afghanistan, India, Pakistan, Bangladesh, Sri Lanka, Nepal, Bhutan and Maldives as the constituent countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_World_Bank_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_World_Bank-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eldis.org_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-eldis.org-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-southasiainstitute.harvard.edu_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-southasiainstitute.harvard.edu-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_BBC._Afghanistan_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_BBC._Afghanistan-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Brookings_Institution_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Brookings_Institution-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_The_World_Factbook_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_The_World_Factbook-24">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> is included by some scholars in South Asia, but in Southeast Asia by others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Razzaque3_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Razzaque3-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mittal_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-mittal-25">[24]</a></sup> Some do not include Afghanistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Razzaque3_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Razzaque3-16">[15]</a></sup> others question whether Afghanistan should be considered a part of South Asia or the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The current territories of <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, which were the core of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> from 1857 to 1947, form the central region of South Asia, in addition to Afghanistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_World_Bank_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_World_Bank-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eldis.org_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-eldis.org-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-southasiainstitute.harvard.edu_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-southasiainstitute.harvard.edu-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_BBC._Afghanistan_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_BBC._Afghanistan-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Brookings_Institution_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Brookings_Institution-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_The_World_Factbook_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_The_World_Factbook-24">[23]</a></sup> which was a British protectorate until 1919, after the Afghans lost to the British in the Second Anglo-Afghan war. The mountain countries of <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, and the island countries of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a> are generally included as well. <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> (formerly Burma) is often added, and by various deviating definitions based on often substantially different reasons, the <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Ocean_Territory" title="British Indian Ocean Territory">British Indian Ocean Territory</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tibet_Autonomous_Region" title="Tibet Autonomous Region">Tibet Autonomous Region</a> are included as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghosh1989_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ghosh1989-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2002_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2002-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The common concept of South Asia is largely inherited from the administrative boundaries of the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> with several exceptions. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aden_Colony" title="Aden Colony">Aden Colony</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Somaliland" title="British Somaliland">British Somaliland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, though administered at various times under the Raj, have not been proposed as any part of South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> Additionally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a> was administered as part of the Raj until 1937, but is now considered a part of Southeast Asia and is a member state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ASEAN" title="ASEAN">ASEAN</a>. The 562 <a href="/wiki/Princely_state" title="Princely state">princely states</a> that were protected by but not directly ruled by the Raj became administrative parts of South Asia upon joining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_of_India" title="Union of India">Union of India</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_Pakistan" title="Dominion of Pakistan">Dominion of Pakistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">Geopolitically</a>, it had formed the whole territory of <a href="/wiki/Greater_India" title="Greater India">Greater India</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mittal_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-mittal-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kathleen_M_page_10_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kathleen_M_page_10-41">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_Association_for_Regional_Cooperation" title="South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation">South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation</a> (SAARC), a contiguous block of countries, started in 1985 with seven countries&#160;&#8211; Bangladesh, Bhutan, India, the Maldives, Nepal, Pakistan and Sri Lanka&#160;&#8211; and added <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> as an eighth member in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and Myanmar have also applied for the status of full members of SAARC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> This bloc of countries include two independent countries that were not part of the British Raj &#8211; Nepal, and Bhutan. Afghanistan was a British protectorate from 1878 until 1919, after the Afghans lost to the British in the Second Anglo-Afghan war. <a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">The World Factbook</a>, based on geo-politics, people, and economy defines South Asia as comprising Afghanistan, Bangladesh, Bhutan, British Indian Ocean Territory, India, Maldives, Nepal, Pakistan, and Sri Lanka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Asia_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="South Asia Free Trade Agreement">South Asia Free Trade Agreement</a> incorporated Afghanistan in 2011, and the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> grouping of countries in the region also includes all eight members comprising South Asia and SAARC as well,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> and the same goes for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Children%27s_Fund" title="United Nations Children\'s Fund">United Nations Children\'s Fund</a> (UNICEF).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:240px; border-width: 1px; font-size: 70%; background-color: #FFFFE0;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8220; ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8221; ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-title" style="background-color: #FFFFE0;">Definition by South Asian Studies programs</div>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">When the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre_for_South_Asian_Studies" title="Centre for South Asian Studies">Centre for South Asian Studies</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a> was established, in 1964, it promoted the study of India, Pakistan, Sri Lanka, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> the Himalayan Kingdoms (<a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, and Sikkim<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup>), and Burma (now Myanmar). It has since included <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>\n<p>The Centres for South Asian Studies at both the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a> include Tibet along with the eight members of SAARC in their research programs, but exclude the Maldives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> The South Asian Studies Program of <a href="/wiki/Rutgers_University" title="Rutgers University">Rutgers University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a> Centre for South Asia Studies also include the Maldives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berkeley_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berkeley-60">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The South Asian Studies Program of <a href="/wiki/Brandeis_University" title="Brandeis University">Brandeis University</a> defines the region as comprising "India, Pakistan, Bangladesh, Sri Lanka, Nepal, Bhutan, and in certain contexts Afghanistan, Burma, Maldives and Tibet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> The similar program of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> includes Afghanistan, Bangladesh, India, the Maldives, Nepal, Pakistan, and Sri Lanka in their study and excludes Burma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indology" title="Indology">Indology</a></div></div>\n</div>\n<p>The United Nations Statistics Division\'s <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_geoscheme" title="United Nations geoscheme">scheme of sub-regions</a> include all eight members of the SAARC as part of Southern Asia, along with <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> only for statistical purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Millenniumindicators.un.org_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Millenniumindicators.un.org-64">[63]</a></sup> Population Information Network (POPIN) includes Afghanistan, Bangladesh, Burma, India, Nepal, Pakistan and Sri Lanka as part of South Asia. Maldives, in view of its characteristics, was admitted as a member Pacific POPIN subregional network only in principle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Herfindahl_index" title="Herfindahl index">Hirschman&#8211;Herfindahl index</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Economic_and_Social_Commission_for_Asia_and_the_Pacific" title="United Nations Economic and Social Commission for Asia and the Pacific">United Nations Economic and Social Commission for Asia and the Pacific</a> for the region includes only the original seven signatories of SAARC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Ocean_Territory" title="British Indian Ocean Territory">British Indian Ocean Territory</a> is connected to the region by a publication of <a href="/wiki/Jane%27s_Information_Group" title="Jane\'s Information Group">Jane\'s</a> for security considerations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> The region may also include the disputed territory of <a href="/wiki/Aksai_Chin" title="Aksai Chin">Aksai Chin</a>, which was part of the British Indian princely state of <a href="/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir_(princely_state)" title="Jammu and Kashmir (princely state)">Jammu and Kashmir</a>, but is now administered as part of the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">Chinese</a> autonomous region of <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The inclusion of Myanmar in South Asia is without consensus, with many considering it a part of Southeast Asia and others including it within South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Razzaque3_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Razzaque3-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mittal_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-mittal-25">[24]</a></sup> Afghanistan was of importance to the British colonial empire, especially after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="Second Anglo-Afghan War">Second Anglo-Afghan War</a> over 1878&#8211;1880. Afghanistan remained a British protectorate until 1919, when a treaty with Vladimir Lenin included the granting of independence to Afghanistan. Following <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_India" title="Partition of India">India\'s partition</a>, Afghanistan has generally been included in South Asia, with some considering it a part of Southwest Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghosh1989_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ghosh1989-14">[13]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War" title="Soviet&#8211;Afghan War">Soviet&#8211;Afghan War</a> (1979&#8211;1989) American foreign policy considered Pakistan and Afghanistan in Southwest Asia, while others included it as a part of South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unreports.studycenters_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-unreports.studycenters-3">[3]</a></sup> There is no universal agreement among scholars on which countries should be included within South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Razzaque3_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Razzaque3-16">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the past, a lack of a coherent definition for South Asia resulted in not only a lack of academic studies, but also in a lack interest for such studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> The confusion existed also because of the lack of a clear boundary &#8211; geographically, geopolitical, socio-culturally, economically or historically &#8211; between South Asia and other parts of Asia, especially the Middle East and Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> Identification with a South Asian identity was also found to be significantly low among respondents in an older two-year survey across Bangladesh, India, Nepal, Pakistan, and Sri Lanka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> However, modern definitions of South Asia are very consistent in including Afghanistan, India, Pakistan, Bangladesh, Sri Lanka, Nepal, Bhutan and Maldives as the constituent countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_World_Bank_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_World_Bank-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eldis.org_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-eldis.org-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-southasiainstitute.harvard.edu_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-southasiainstitute.harvard.edu-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_BBC._Afghanistan_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_BBC._Afghanistan-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Brookings_Institution_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_Brookings_Institution-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_The_World_Factbook_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_The_World_Factbook-24">[23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_Association_for_Regional_Cooperation" title="South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation">South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_Free_Trade_Area" title="South Asian Free Trade Area">South Asian Free Trade Area</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Indian subcontinent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a></div>\n<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a>, the term "subcontinent" signifies a "subdivision of a continent which has a distinct geographical, political, or cultural identity" and also a "large land mass somewhat smaller than a continent".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> Historians Catherine Asher and Cynthia Talbot state that the term "Indian subcontinent" describes a natural physical landmass in South Asia that has been relatively isolated from the rest of Eurasia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsherTalbot2006_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-AsherTalbot2006-74">[73]</a></sup> The Indian subcontinent is also a geological term referring to the land mass that drifted northeastwards from ancient <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a>, colliding with the Eurasian plate nearly 55 million years ago, towards the end of Palaeocene. This geological region largely includes Bangladesh, Bhutan, India, Maldives, Nepal, Pakistan and Sri Lanka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2011p267_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2011p267-75">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The use of the term Indian subcontinent began in the British Empire, and has been a term particularly common in its successors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcleodplus_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcleodplus-76">[75]</a></sup> This region has also been labelled as "India" (in its classical and <a href="/wiki/Middle_kingdoms_of_India" title="Middle kingdoms of India">pre-modern</a> sense), "Greater India", or as South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mittal_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-mittal-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kathleen_M_page_10_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kathleen_M_page_10-41">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to anthropologist <a href="/wiki/John_R._Lukacs" title="John R. Lukacs">John R. Lukacs</a>, "the Indian Subcontinent occupies the major landmass of South Asia",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> while the political science professor <a href="/wiki/Tatu_Vanhanen" title="Tatu Vanhanen">Tatu Vanhanen</a> states, "the seven countries of South Asia constitute geographically a compact region around the Indian Subcontinent".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> According to Chris Brewster, India, Pakistan, Bangladesh, Sri Lanka, Nepal and Bhutan constitute the Indian subcontinent; with Afghanistan and Maldives included it is more commonly referred to as South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> The geopolitical boundaries of Indian subcontinent, according to Dhavendra Kumar, include "India, Pakistan, Bangladesh, Sri Lanka, Nepal, Bhutan and other small islands of the Indian Ocean".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> Maldives, the country consisting of a small <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> southwest of the peninsula, is considered part of the Indian subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The terms "Indian subcontinent" and "South Asia" are sometimes used interchangeably.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2002_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2002-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcleodplus_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-mcleodplus-76">[75]</a></sup> The South Asia term is particularly common when scholars or officials seek to differentiate this region from East Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inden_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-inden-82">[81]</a></sup> According to historians <a href="/wiki/Sugata_Bose" title="Sugata Bose">Sugata Bose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayesha_Jalal" title="Ayesha Jalal">Ayesha Jalal</a>, the Indian subcontinent has come to be known as South Asia "in more recent and neutral parlance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sugata_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-sugata-83">[82]</a></sup> This "neutral" notion refers to the concerns of Pakistan and Bangladesh, particularly given the recurring conflicts between India and Pakistan, wherein the dominant placement of "India" as a prefix before the subcontinent might offend some political sentiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mittal_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-mittal-25">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is no globally accepted definition on which countries are a part of South Asia or Indian subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Razzaque3_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Razzaque3-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mmann_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-mmann-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson5_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-anderson5-18">[17]</a></sup> While Afghanistan is not considered as a part of the Indian subcontinent, Afghanistan is often included in South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson5_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-anderson5-18">[17]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> is included by some scholars in South Asia but not in Indian subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mittal_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-mittal-25">[24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_South_Asia" title="History of South Asia">History of South Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">History of India</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_era">Ancient era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ancient era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The history of core South Asia begins with evidence of human activity of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">Homo sapiens</a>,</i> as long as 75,000 years ago, or with earlier hominids including <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> from about 500,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization" title="Indus Valley Civilization">Indus Valley Civilization</a>, which spread and flourished in the northwestern part of South Asia from c. 3300 to 1300 BCE in present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_India" title="Northern India">Northern India</a>, Pakistan and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, was the first major civilization in South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> A sophisticated and technologically advanced urban culture developed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mature_Harappan" title="Mature Harappan">Mature Harappan</a> period, from 2600 to 1900 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The earliest prehistoric culture have roots in the mesolithic sites as evidenced by the rock paintings of <a href="/wiki/Bhimbetka_rock_shelters" title="Bhimbetka rock shelters">Bhimbetka rock shelters</a> dating to a period of 30,000 BCE or older,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[note 2]</a></sup> as well as neolithic times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[note 3]</a></sup> According to anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Possehl" title="Gregory Possehl">Possehl</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization" title="Indus Valley Civilization">Indus Valley Civilization</a> provides a logical, if somewhat arbitrary, starting point for South Asian religions, but these links from the Indus religion to later-day South Asian traditions are subject to scholarly dispute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPossehl2002141&#8211;156_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPossehl2002141&#8211;156-89">[86]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shiva,_Vishnu,_and_Brahma_Adoring_Kali_LACMA_M.80.101_(2_of_7).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1553" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Shiva%2C_Vishnu%2C_and_Brahma_Adoring_Kali_LACMA_M.80.101_%282_of_7%29.jpg/190px-Shiva%2C_Vishnu%2C_and_Brahma_Adoring_Kali_LACMA_M.80.101_%282_of_7%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Shiva%2C_Vishnu%2C_and_Brahma_Adoring_Kali_LACMA_M.80.101_%282_of_7%29.jpg/285px-Shiva%2C_Vishnu%2C_and_Brahma_Adoring_Kali_LACMA_M.80.101_%282_of_7%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Shiva%2C_Vishnu%2C_and_Brahma_Adoring_Kali_LACMA_M.80.101_%282_of_7%29.jpg/380px-Shiva%2C_Vishnu%2C_and_Brahma_Adoring_Kali_LACMA_M.80.101_%282_of_7%29.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shiva,_Vishnu,_and_Brahma_Adoring_Kali_LACMA_M.80.101_(2_of_7).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Trimurti" title="Trimurti">Trimurti</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Triple_deity" title="Triple deity">trinity</a> of <a href="/wiki/Para_Brahman" title="Para Brahman">supreme divinity</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, typically <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a> the creator, <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> the preserver, and <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a> the destroyer</div></div></div>\n<p>The Vedic period, named after the Vedic religion of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_peoples" title="Indo-Aryan peoples">Indo-Aryans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[note 4]</a></sup> lasted from c. 1900 to 500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200432_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200432-92">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel19953-4_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel19953-4-93">[89]</a></sup> The Indo-Aryans were pastoralists<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel1995_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel1995-94">[90]</a></sup> who migrated into north-western India after the collapse of the Indus Valley Civilization,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200433_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200433-90">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199630-35_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199630-35-95">[91]</a></sup> Linguistic and archaeological data show a cultural change after 1500 BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichaels200433_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichaels200433-90">[87]</a></sup> with the linguistic and religious data clearly showing links with Indo-European languages and religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199633_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199633-96">[92]</a></sup> By about 1200 BCE, the Vedic culture and agrarian lifestyle was established in the northwest and northern Gangetic plain of South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel1995_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel1995-94">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuel201041-48_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuel201041-48-97">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStein201048-49_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStein201048-49-98">[94]</a></sup> Rudimentary state-forms appeared, of which the <a href="/wiki/Kuru_Kingdom" title="Kuru Kingdom">Kuru</a>-Pa&#241;c&#257;la union was the most influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel19956_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel19956-99">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuel201051-53_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuel201051-53-100">[96]</a></sup> The first recorded <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state-level society</a> in South Asia existed around 1000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel1995_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel1995-94">[90]</a></sup> In this period, states Samuel, emerged the Brahmana and Aranyaka layers of Vedic texts, which merged into the earliest Upanishads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuel201025_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuel201025-101">[97]</a></sup> These texts began to ask the meaning of a ritual, adding increasing levels of philosophical and metaphysical speculation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuel201025_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuel201025-101">[97]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Hinduism#Roots_of_Hinduism" title="Hinduism">"Hindu synthesis"</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel200712_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel200712-102">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Increasing urbanisation of India between 800 and 400 BCE, and possibly the spread of urban diseases, contributed to the rise of ascetic movements and of new ideas which challenged the orthodox <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmanism" title="Brahmanism">Brahmanism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199681&#8211;82_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199681&#8211;82-103">[99]</a></sup> These ideas led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sramana" title="Sramana">Sramana</a> movements, of which Mahavira (c. 549&#8211;477 BCE), proponent of <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> (c. 563-483), founder of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, were the most prominent icons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Religions_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_Religions-104">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Greek army led by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> stayed in the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> region of South Asia for several years and then later moved into the Indus valley region. Later, the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya Empire</a> extended over much of South Asia in the 3rd century BCE. Buddhism spread beyond south Asia, through northwest into Central Asia. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bamiyan_Buddhas" title="Bamiyan Buddhas">Bamiyan Buddhas</a> of Afghanistan and the <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">edicts of A&#347;oka</a> suggest that the Buddhist monks spread Buddhism (Dharma) in eastern provinces of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, and possibly even farther into West Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich2006135_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich2006135-105">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrainor2004103,_119_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrainor2004103,_119-106">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup> The Theravada school spread south from India in the 3rd century BCE, to Sri Lanka, later to Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[104]</a></sup> Buddhism, by the last centuries of the 1st millennium BCE, was prominent in the Himalayan region, Gandhara, Hindu Kush region and Bactria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neelis2010p249_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neelis2010p249-109">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deborahkh_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-deborahkh-110">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[107]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From about 500 BCE through about 300 CE, the Vedic-Brahmanic synthesis or "Hindu synthesis" continued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel200712_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel200712-102">[98]</a></sup> Classical Hindu and Sramanic (particularly Buddhist) ideas spread within South Asia, as well outside South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuel2010193&#8211;228,_339&#8211;353,_specifically_pp.&amp;nbsp;76&#8211;79_and_194&#8211;199_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuel2010193&#8211;228,_339&#8211;353,_specifically_pp.&amp;nbsp;76&#8211;79_and_194&#8211;199-113">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[110]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gupta_Empire" title="Gupta Empire">Gupta Empire</a> ruled over a large part of the region between 4th and 7th centuries, a period that saw the construction of major temples, monasteries and universities such as the <a href="/wiki/Nalanda" title="Nalanda">Nalanda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichell197718,_40_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichell197718,_40-115">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scharfe2002p144_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-scharfe2002p144-116">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[113]</a></sup> During this era, and through the 10th century, numerous cave monasteries and temples such as the <a href="/wiki/Ajanta_Caves" title="Ajanta Caves">Ajanta Caves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Badami_cave_temples" title="Badami cave temples">Badami cave temples</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ellora_Caves" title="Ellora Caves">Ellora Caves</a> were built in South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[116]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_era">Medieval era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Medieval era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Timur_defeats_the_sultan_of_Delhi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="722" data-file-width="430" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Timur_defeats_the_sultan_of_Delhi.jpg/190px-Timur_defeats_the_sultan_of_Delhi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Timur_defeats_the_sultan_of_Delhi.jpg/285px-Timur_defeats_the_sultan_of_Delhi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Timur_defeats_the_sultan_of_Delhi.jpg/380px-Timur_defeats_the_sultan_of_Delhi.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Timur_defeats_the_sultan_of_Delhi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Turco-Mongol conqueror <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> defeats the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultan_of_Delhi" title="Sultan of Delhi">Sultan of Delhi</a>, Nasir-u Din Mehmud, in the winter of 1397&#8211;1398</div></div></div>\n<p>Islam came as a political power in the fringe of South Asia in 8th century CE when the Arab general <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_bin_Qasim" title="Muhammad bin Qasim">Muhammad bin Qasim</a> conquered <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Multan" title="Multan">Multan</a> in southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a> in modern-day Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infopak_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-infopak-121">[117]</a></sup> By 962 CE, Hindu and Buddhist kingdoms in South Asia were under a wave of raids from Muslim armies from Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mrpislam_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-mrpislam-122">[118]</a></sup> Among them was <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_of_Ghazni" title="Mahmud of Ghazni">Mahmud of Ghazni</a>, who raided and plundered kingdoms in north India from east of the Indus river to west of Yamuna river seventeen times between 997 and 1030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pj03_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-pj03-123">[119]</a></sup> Mahmud of Ghazni raided the treasuries but retracted each time, only extending Islamic rule into western Punjab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[121]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The wave of raids on north Indian and western Indian kingdoms by Muslim warlords continued after Mahmud of Ghazni, plundering and looting these kingdoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[122]</a></sup> The raids did not establish or extend permanent boundaries of their Islamic kingdoms. The Ghurid Sultan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu%27izz_al-Din_Muhammad" title="Mu\'izz al-Din Muhammad">Mu\'izz al-Din Muhammad</a> began a systematic war of expansion into north India in 1173.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[123]</a></sup> He sought to carve out a principality for himself by expanding the Islamic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pj03_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-pj03-123">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[124]</a></sup> Mu\'izz sought a <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islamic</a> kingdom of his own extending east of the Indus river, and he thus laid the foundation for the Muslim kingdom that became the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pj03_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-pj03-123">[119]</a></sup> Some historians chronicle the Delhi Sultanate from 1192 due to the presence and geographical claims of Mu\'izz al-Din in South Asia by that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[125]</a></sup> The Delhi Sultanate covered varying parts of South Asia, and was ruled by a series of dynasties, called Mamluk, Khalji, Tughlaq, Sayyid and Lodi dynasties. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_bin_Tughlaq" title="Muhammad bin Tughlaq">Muhammad bin Tughlaq</a> came to power in 1325, launched a war of expansion and the Delhi Sultanate reached it largest geographical reach over the South Asian region during his 26-year rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebmit_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-ebmit-130">[126]</a></sup> A Sunni Sultan, Muhammad bin Tughlaq persecuted non-Muslims such as Hindus, as well as non-Sunni Muslims such as Shia and Mahdi sects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vsoxfordmbt_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-vsoxfordmbt-132">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[129]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Revolts against the Delhi Sultanate sprang up in many parts of South Asia during the 14th century. After the death of Muhammad bin Tughlaq, the Bengal Sultanate came to power in 1352 CE, as the Delhi Sultanate began disintegrating. The Bengal Sultanate remained in power through the early 16th century. It was reconquered by the armies of the Mughal Empire. The state religion of the Bengal Sultanate was Islam, and the region under its rule, a region that ultimately emerged as the modern nation of Bangladesh, saw a growth of a syncretic form of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis2011_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2011-134">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussain2003_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hussain2003-135">[131]</a></sup> In the Deccan region, the Hindu kingdom <a href="/wiki/Vijayanagara_Empire" title="Vijayanagara Empire">Vijayanagara Empire</a> came to power in 1336 and remained in power through the 16th century, after which it too was reconquered and absorbed into the Mughal Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[133]</a></sup>\n</p><p>About 1526, the Punjab governor Dawlat Khan Lod&#299; reached out to the Mughal <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> and invited him to attack Delhi Sultanate. <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> defeated and killed Ibrahim Lodi in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Panipat_(1526)" title="Battle of Panipat (1526)">Battle of Panipat</a> in 1526. The death of Ibrahim Lodi ended the Delhi Sultanate, and the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> replaced it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eblodi_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-eblodi-138">[134]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Modern_history" title="Modern history">modern history</a> period of South Asia, that is 16th-century onwards, witnessed the start of the Central Asian dynasty named the Mughals, with Turkish-Mongol roots and Sunni Islam theology. The first ruler was Babur, whose empire extended the northwest and <a href="/wiki/Indo-Gangetic_Plain" title="Indo-Gangetic Plain">Indo-Gangetic Plain</a> regions of South Asia. The Deccan and northeastern region of the South Asia was largely under Hindu kings such as those of Vijayanagara Empire and <a href="/wiki/Ahom_kingdom" title="Ahom kingdom">Ahom kingdom</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[135]</a></sup> with some regions such as parts of modern <a href="/wiki/Telangana" title="Telangana">Telangana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh" title="Andhra Pradesh">Andhra Pradesh</a> under local Sultanates such as the Shia Islamic rulers of <a href="/wiki/Qutb_Shahi_dynasty" title="Qutb Shahi dynasty">Golconda Sultanate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[136]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_Indian_Empire_1909_Imperial_Gazetteer_of_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1223" data-file-width="1498" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/British_Indian_Empire_1909_Imperial_Gazetteer_of_India.jpg/220px-British_Indian_Empire_1909_Imperial_Gazetteer_of_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/British_Indian_Empire_1909_Imperial_Gazetteer_of_India.jpg/330px-British_Indian_Empire_1909_Imperial_Gazetteer_of_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/British_Indian_Empire_1909_Imperial_Gazetteer_of_India.jpg/440px-British_Indian_Empire_1909_Imperial_Gazetteer_of_India.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_Indian_Empire_1909_Imperial_Gazetteer_of_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Indian Empire in 1909. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_India" title="British India">British India</a> is shaded pink, the <a href="/wiki/Princely_state" title="Princely state">princely states</a> yellow.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Mughal Empire continued its wars of expansion after Babur\'s death. With the fall of Rajput kingdoms and Vijayanagara, its boundaries reached all of west, as well as the Marathi and Kannada speaking regions of the Deccan peninsula. The Mughal Empire was marked by a period of artistic exchanges and a Central Asian and South Asian architecture synthesis, with remarkable buildings such as the <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[137]</a></sup> It also marked an extended period of <a href="/wiki/Religious_violence_in_India#Mughal_Empire" title="Religious violence in India">religious persecution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[138]</a></sup> Two of the religious leaders of <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Arjan" title="Guru Arjan">Guru Arjan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guru_Tegh_Bahadur" title="Guru Tegh Bahadur">Guru Tegh Bahadur</a> were arrested under orders of the Mughal emperors, asked to convert to Islam, and executed when they refused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[141]</a></sup> Religious taxes on non-Muslims called <i>jizya</i> were imposed. Buddhist, Hindu and Sikh temples were desecrated. However, not all Muslim rulers persecuted non-Muslims. <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a>, a Mughal ruler for example, sought religious tolerance and abolished jizya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[142]</a></sup> After his death, the persecution of non-Muslims in South Asia returned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[143]</a></sup> The persecution and religious violence in South Asia peaked during Aurangzeb era, with him issuing orders in 1669, to all his governors of provinces to "destroy with a willing hand the schools and temples of the infidels, and that they were strictly enjoined to put an entire stop to the teaching and practice of idolatrous forms of worship".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[145]</a></sup> In Aurangzeb\'s time, almost all of South Asia was claimed by the Mughal Empire. However, this claim was violently challenged in various regions of South Asia, particularly by the Sikh <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a> in the northwest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[146]</a></sup> and by <a href="/wiki/Shivaji" title="Shivaji">Shivaji</a> in the Deccan regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[147]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Maritime trading between South Asia and European merchants began after the Portuguese explorer Vasco de Gama returned to Europe. After the death of Aurangzeb and the collapse of the Mughal Empire, the region came under the rule of many small Islamic sultanates and Hindu kingdoms. British, French, Portuguese colonial interests struck treaties with these rulers, and established their trading ports. In northwest South Asia, a large region was consolidated into the Sikh Empire by <a href="/wiki/Ranjit_Singh" title="Ranjit Singh">Ranjit Singh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[149]</a></sup> After his death, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> expanded their interests till the Hindu Kush region. In the east, the Bengal region was split into Muslim East Bengal and Hindu West Bengal, by the colonial British empire, in early 1900s, a split that was reversed. However, after the World War II, at the eve of India\'s independence, the region was split again into East Pakistan and West Bengal. East Pakistan became Bangladesh in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[151]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_India" title="Geography of India">Geography of India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Pakistan" title="Geography of Pakistan">Geography of Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Afghanistan" title="Geography of Afghanistan">Geography of Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Bangladesh" title="Geography of Bangladesh">Geography of Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Bhutan" title="Geography of Bhutan">Geography of Bhutan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Sri_Lanka" title="Geography of Sri Lanka">Geography of Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Nepal" title="Geography of Nepal">Geography of Nepal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_the_Maldives" title="Geography of the Maldives">Geography of the Maldives</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indian_subcontinent.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="545" data-file-width="442" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Indian_subcontinent.JPG/220px-Indian_subcontinent.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Indian_subcontinent.JPG/330px-Indian_subcontinent.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Indian_subcontinent.JPG/440px-Indian_subcontinent.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indian_subcontinent.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>While South Asia had never been a coherent <a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">geopolitical</a> region, it has a distinct geographical identity</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Himalaya-formation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="234" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Himalaya-formation.gif/170px-Himalaya-formation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/Himalaya-formation.gif 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Himalaya-formation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Indian peninsula, and the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> on the northeast, is the result of the collision of the Indian Plate with the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian Plate</a> through <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">tectonic activity</a> between 20 and 50 million years ago.</div></div></div>\n<p>According to Saul Cohen, early colonial era strategists treated South Asia with East Asia, but in reality the South Asia region excluding Afghanistan is a distinct <a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">geopolitical</a> region separated from other nearby geostrategic realms, one that is geographically diverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[152]</a></sup> The region is home to a variety of geographical features, such as <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforests</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valley" title="Valley">valleys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">deserts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grasslands</a> that are typical of much larger continents. It is surrounded by three water bodies&#160;&#8211; the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a>&#160;&#8211; and has acutely varied climate zones. The tip of the Indian Peninsula had the highest quality pearls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[153]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boundary">Boundary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Boundary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The boundaries of South Asia vary based on how the region is defined. South Asia\'s northern, eastern, and western boundaries vary based on definitions used, while the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> is the southern periphery. Most of this region rests on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian Plate</a> and is isolated from the rest of Asia by mountain barriers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[155]</a></sup> Much of the region consists of a <a href="/wiki/Peninsula" title="Peninsula">peninsula</a> in south-central Asia, rather resembling a diamond which is delineated by the Himalayas on the north, the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> in the west, and the <a href="/wiki/Chin_Hills" title="Chin Hills">Arakanese</a> in the east,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[156]</a></sup> and which extends southward into the Indian Ocean with the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a> to the southwest and the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> to the southeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLeod2002_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-McLeod2002-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-30">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to Robert M. Cutler &#8211; a scholar of Political Science at <a href="/wiki/Carleton_University" title="Carleton University">Carleton University</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amineh2007p112_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amineh2007p112-161">[157]</a></sup> the terms South Asia, Southwest Asia and Central Asia are distinct, but the confusion and disagreements have arisen due to the geopolitical movement to enlarge these regions into Greater South Asia, Greater Southwest Asia and Greater Central Asia. The frontier of Greater South Asia, states Cutler, between 2001&#8211;2006 has been geopolitically extended to eastern Iran and western Afghanistan in the west, and in the north to northeastern Iran, northern Afghanistan, and southern <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amineh2007p112_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Amineh2007p112-161">[157]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_plate">Indian plate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Indian plate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_plate" title="Indian plate">Indian plate</a></div>\n<p>Most of this region is resting on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian Plate</a>, the northerly portion of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Australian_Plate" title="Indo-Australian Plate">Indo-Australian Plate</a>, separated from the rest of the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian Plate</a>. The Indian Plate includes most of South Asia, forming a land mass which extends from the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> into a portion of the basin under the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, including parts of <a href="/wiki/Southwest_China" title="Southwest China">South China</a> and Eastern <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Kunlun_Mountains" title="Kunlun Mountains">Kunlun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karakoram" title="Karakoram">Karakoram</a> ranges,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[159]</a></sup> and extending up to but not including <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kohistan_District,_Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa" title="Kohistan District, Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa">Kohistan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> range and <a href="/wiki/Balochistan" title="Balochistan">Balochistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[162]</a></sup> It may be noted that <a href="/wiki/Geophysics" title="Geophysics">geophysically</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yarlung_Tsangpo_River" title="Yarlung Tsangpo River">Yarlung Tsangpo River</a> in Tibet is situated at the outside of the border of the regional structure, while the <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir Mountains</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a> are situated inside that border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[163]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was once a small <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continent</a> before colliding with the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian Plate</a> about 50&#8211;55 million years ago and giving birth to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himalayan_range" title="Himalayan range">Himalayan range</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibetan_plateau" title="Tibetan plateau">Tibetan plateau</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/Peninsula" title="Peninsula">peninsular</a> region south of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himalaya" title="Himalaya">Himalayas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuen_Lun" title="Kuen Lun">Kuen Lun</a> mountain ranges and east of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Plateau" title="Iranian Plateau">Iranian Plateau</a>, extending southward into the Indian Ocean between the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a> (to the southwest) and the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> (to the southeast).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:South_Asia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1630" data-file-width="2760" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/South_Asia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/350px-South_Asia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/South_Asia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/525px-South_Asia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/South_Asia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/700px-South_Asia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:South_Asia_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>South Asia\'s <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K&#246;ppen climate classification">K&#246;ppen climate classification</a> map<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peel_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peel-168">[164]</a></sup> is based on native vegetation, temperature, precipitation and their seasonality. <div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 11em; -webkit-column-width: 11em; column-width: 11em;"><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" <b>Tropical savanna, wet &amp; dry</b></a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold_semi-arid_climate" title="Cold semi-arid climate">(BSk) Cold semi arid</a></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFFF00; color:black;
